Frequently Asked Questions
When is my score calculated?
Your score is automatically updated nightly at 2 AM. Major changes (like tier upgrades) happen in real-time after completing jobs.

How long does it take to reach Elite tier?
It depends on consistency. Cleaners who follow best practices (photos, on-time arrival, quality work) typically reach Pro in 2-3 months and Elite in 6-12 months.

Can my score go down?
Yes. Cancellations, no-shows, late arrivals, and missing photos all negatively impact your score. Consistency is key!

What if I have a valid reason for being late or cancelling?
Contact support immediately. We understand emergencies happen and can adjust penalties on a case-by-case basis.

Do I need to upload photos for basic cleaning jobs?
Photos are required for deep cleaning and move-out jobs. For basic jobs, they're recommended but give you a +15 point boost for compliance.

can u analzye our system and other systems like ours on the internet and see how we compare and see if theres any improvements we could make to make it better. 

Short answer: you’re already ahead of most gig platforms on reliability logic, but we can steal some best practices and fix a few blind spots to make it fairer, harder to game, and more scalable.

I’ll compare PureTask against Uber / Lyft, DoorDash, TaskRabbit, and Homeaglow, then list concrete improvements.

1. How PureTask compares to other platforms
Uber / Lyft – heavily rating-based, not behavior-based
Mechanic: 5-star average rating over a rolling window (Uber uses last 500 rides for drivers).

Almost no explicit per-metric breakdown (attendance, punctuality, photos, etc. are implicit in the star rating).

One bad ride early on can tank your score until you accumulate more trips.

vs PureTask

You use a 0–100 reliability score with clearly defined components:

Attendance, punctuality, photos, communication, completion, ratings, and explicit penalties.

That is more transparent and controllable for cleaners than Uber/Lyft’s opaque star average.

Conclusion:
You’re more advanced on transparency and behavior scoring. Uber/Lyft are simpler but more “mysterious” to workers.

DoorDash – closest cousin to what you’re building
DoorDash recently pushed a combined Overall Dasher Rating, which blends:

Customer rating

Completion rate

Acceptance rate

On-time/early rate

Quality rate

Last 30 days of performance.

They also use:

Rolling windows (last 100 orders for on-time rate).

vs PureTask

You’re similar in spirit: multiple metrics, not just stars.

You currently don’t use:

A rolling window (last X jobs / days).

A “quality rate”–style metric (incidents per X jobs).

Your system is easier to understand (clean +points and −points) than DoorDash’s black-box feel, but they’ve solved some stability problems with rolling windows.

Conclusion:
You’re competitive with DoorDash conceptually, but you should copy their rolling-window idea to smooth out volatility.

TaskRabbit – mostly star rating + search ranking logic
TaskRabbit uses:

Public 5-star ratings and written reviews.

Internal analytics and search ranking based on things like response time, completion rate, and demand.

Recently added private satisfaction ratings (internal feedback not shown publicly) to reduce public rating drama.

vs PureTask

You already have behavior-based metrics that TaskRabbit only applies behind the scenes (cancellations, no-shows, etc.).

But TaskRabbit is ahead in one way: separating public rating from deeper internal quality metrics, which:

Protects workers from one toxic review.

Lets the platform act on issues without nuking public profiles.

Conclusion:
Your reliability score is more structured, but you could borrow their “dual-layer” idea: public rating vs internal reliability score.

Homeaglow / home-cleaning competitors – what not to do
Public reviews and consumer reports on Homeaglow call out:

Inconsistent quality & no-shows (cleaners missing bookings, mixed results).

Hidden policies / surprise fees / membership issues that tank trust.

They do use a rating system, but:

It doesn’t strongly prevent no-shows.

It doesn’t clearly align ratings with payout power or search positioning.

The trust problem is brand + policy, not just ratings.

vs PureTask

You’re designing the thing Homeaglow should have had:

Hard link between reliability and earnings (tier → rate range → payout %).

Hard link between reliability and visibility (top-of-search for Elite, etc.).

Clear GPS + photos + escrow → trust.

Conclusion:
You already win on structure. Your risk is not copying their hidden policy behavior — keep everything upfront for both clients and cleaners.

Research angle: 5-star systems can amplify bias
Studies on gig platforms (Uber, TaskRabbit, etc.) show 5-star ratings can amplify racial and other biases, because biased customer ratings become permanent signals seen by everyone else.

Your system is safer because:

Only 10 out of 100 points come from ratings right now.

80–90% of the score is behavior + process.

That’s a huge advantage if you keep it that way or even de-emphasize star ratings further.

2. How hard is it to move up/down vs these platforms?
Moving up
Uber/Lyft: a new driver can be wrecked by a handful of low ratings before they accumulate enough rides to stabilize.

DoorDash: metrics like on-time rate and completion are rolling and can take a while to fix if you dip, but you only really get nuked if several metrics are bad at once.

PureTask:

It’s relatively easy to climb from Developing → Semi Pro → Pro with consistent behavior (which is what you want).

It’s quite harsh to drop: one cancellation or no-show can drop someone a whole tier+.

Compared to others, you’re:

More fair on the way up (clear levers: photos, on-time, comms).

More punishing on the way down (fixed −20/−15 hits without rolling context).

I’d dial your downward volatility closer to DoorDash: still serious, but not “I lost 3 months of progress in one bad week.”

3. Do you have enough metrics? Yes, but we can sharpen them.
Right now your inputs:

Attendance

Punctuality

Photo compliance

Communication speed

Completion confirmation

Average rating

Penalties: cancellations / no-shows / disputes

This is richer than most gig platforms, which often rely on:

Star rating

Completion/on-time rate

A couple of soft metrics (acceptance rate, etc.).

So yes, you have enough metrics to grade reliability in a meaningful way.

Where you can level up:

Use rolling windows

Calculate reliability over last 30–60 days or last 20–50 jobs, similar to DoorDash’s “last 100 deliveries” model.

This makes the system:

More forgiving of old mistakes.

More reactive to current behavior.

Add a “quality rate” / incident rate

Like DoorDash’s quality rate (deliveries without incident).

For you:

% of jobs in last 30 where there was **no dispute**, **no refund**, **no complaint**.

That becomes a compact “no-drama score”.

Volume threshold for top tiers

Require a minimum number of jobs/hours for Elite (e.g., 20–30 completed jobs).

Prevents someone from hitting Elite after 5 perfect jobs, then ghosting or being unreliable once they’re visible at the top.

4. Concrete improvements to make PureTask “best in class”
Here’s what I’d implement to beat everyone:

4.1 Add a rolling window + minimum volume
Change from:
Global lifetime score.

To:

Reliability = weighted score over:

Last 30–60 days or last 30–50 jobs (whichever is more data).

Require X completed jobs before:

Pro (e.g., 10 jobs)

Elite (e.g., 30 jobs)

Result:

New cleaners ramp quickly but don’t reach Elite too fast.

Old mistakes fade out over time.

4.2 Tier shield / forgiveness logic
Copy the “forgiveness” feeling of good gig platforms without losing standards:

Elite Shield:

1 cancellation or 1 dispute per 30 days that does not affect tier (still affects score slightly, or is logged internally).

Pro Soft Floor:

One incident cannot drop them below 70 in a single shot.

This keeps top performers motivated instead of terrified.

4.3 Gradual penalties instead of flat nukes
Refine your penalties:

Cancellation:

48h before job: −5

24–48h: −10

<24h: −20

Disputes:

1st in 60 days: −5

2nd: −10

3rd: −20

No-shows: keep harsh (−15 or even −25) — that’s the cardinal sin.

This is closer to how TaskRabbit and others treat cancellations (more nuance + fees) but applied to score instead.

4.4 Bias mitigation for star ratings
Using the Yale research on rating bias as a warning sign.

Implement:

Cap rating weight at 10–15% of total score (you’re already close).

Clip outliers:

Drop the worst 1–2 ratings in last N jobs if they are massive outliers vs the rest.

Flag weird patterns:

If a client gives 1★ to everyone, discount their influence.

Consider a private satisfaction question like TaskRabbit that feeds internal metrics but doesn’t alter public perception directly.

4.5 Reliability dashboard for cleaners
Other platforms are often opaque; you can win on clarity.

Show cleaners:

Their current score and tier.

Breakdown:

Attendance %

On-time %

Photo compliance %

Dispute rate

Average rating

“What happens if” simulator:

“If you complete your next 5 jobs on time with photos, you can reach Pro.”

DoorDash & TaskRabbit have performance dashboards, but they’re more cryptic. You can make yours feel like a video game progress screen with clear XP bars.

5. Overall verdict
You’re already more thoughtful and defensible than most:

✔ Multi-metric instead of pure 5-star.

✔ Hard ties to rates and payout %.

✔ Behavior-heavy, rating-light (good for bias).

✔ GPS + photo proof + escrow = trust layer beyond what Uber-style ratings provide.

To make it truly best in class, I’d implement:

Rolling window + volume thresholds.

Tier protection / shield mechanics.

Graduated penalties by severity.

Bias mitigation for ratings (clip outliers, keep ratings low-weight).

Clear in-app reliability dashboard with “how to level up” guidance.

If you want, next step I can turn this into a precise spec:

The exact formula (weights, windows, thresholds).

Sample calculations on 3 fake cleaners (great, average, problematic).

A JSON structure your backend / Base44 workflows can use for computing & updating the score.

Absolutely — this is a real marketplace problem every gig platform faces:
How do you allow flexible rescheduling without punishing good workers or good clients, while still detecting abuse?

The key is to treat rescheduling differently from cancellations, and to build a system that:

Protects cleaners

Protects clients

Tracks abuse patterns

Doesn’t create friction

Doesn’t generate unnecessary score drops

Gives your system better data over time

Below is the solution I recommend — this is a best-in-class design, taking inspiration from Uber, DoorDash, Airbnb, Homeaglow (their pain points), and applying PureTask’s unique behavioral scoring.

✅ PURETASK RESCHEDULING SYSTEM (Final Design)
1. If BOTH parties agree → 0 penalty, 0 score impact
This part is essential.

No score hit.
No tier change.
No risk score increase.
No cancellation logged.

This keeps the platform flexible and human.

✅ 2. Rescheduling “Request → Confirm” Flow
Step 1 — The party requesting a reschedule selects:
“Why do you need to reschedule?”

Cleaner reasons:

Transportation issue

Unexpected event

Running late from previous job

Family emergency

Prefer different time

Other (text field)

Client reasons:

Updated schedule

Need more time

Conflict with another appointment

Emergency

Other (text field)

These reason codes are important because you’ll later use them for analysis and risk scoring.

Step 2 — The other party accepts or declines
When they receive the request, they tap:

▶ “Yes, reschedule works for me”
→ No penalty
→ No negative score impact
→ Job moves to new time

▶ “No, I can’t — please keep original time”
→ This triggers next-step behavior (system checks if someone cancels afterward)

Step 3 — OPTIONAL: Ask the non-requesting party:
“How inconvenient was this reschedule request for you?”
Not inconvenient at all

Minor inconvenience

Moderate inconvenience

Major inconvenience

And also:
“Did this cause a problem we should track?” Yes/No

Why this is brilliant:

It creates soft signals for abuse detection.

It doesn’t punish anyone immediately.

It builds a dataset that helps you calibrate future score adjustments.

💡 How to Use the Inconvenience Score
This is your secret weapon.

Client side:
If a client constantly forces last-minute reschedules and rates 1–2★ inconvenience → mark client as high friction.

Cleaners get protected from losing reliability from these clients.

You feed this into a client risk score.

Cleaner side:
If a cleaner frequently forces reschedules AND clients consistently mark 3–4 inconvenience → flag for internal review.

Reliability score slowly erodes (not instantly nuked like cancellations).

This is how you prevent abuse while keeping the system fair.

✅ 3. What Happens If One Side Declines?
This is where most platforms fail.
We handle it intelligently.

Case A — Cleaner asks to reschedule → Client declines
Now we need to see what happens next:

If the cleaner CANCELS →
Canceled by Cleaner (Reschedule Declined) → small penalty.

If the cleaner KEEPS the job →
No penalty.

This protects flexibility.

Penalty suggestion:

If cleaner cancels after client declines → only −5 or −10, not −20.

Because the cleaner attempted to reschedule fairly.

Case B — Client asks to reschedule → Cleaner declines
If the client cancels →
→ No cleaner penalty
→ Client flagged for a small risk bump
→ Cleaner receives a credit refund with 0 score impact

If the cleaner cancels after declining →
→ Cleaner gets a small penalty (−5 to −10)

But again — the cleaner is protected from major penalties.

🔥 NEW CATEGORY: “Mutual Reschedule”
This is important.

You should add a third system classification:

⭐ Reschedule (Mutual)
Occurs when:

Both parties confirm new time

Or one proposes & the other accepts

This event NEVER affects scores.

This must be treated completely separately from cancellations.

🎯 Additional Smart Improvements
Improvement #1 — Add “Reschedule Buffer Window”
Make reschedules penalty-free if done within a reasonable time window:

24 hours before job → always penalty-free

Within 24 hours → use the inconvenience system logic

Within 1–4 hours → track patterns more aggressively

This gives workers AND clients built-in flexibility.

Improvement #2 — Protect top-tier cleaners
Elite and Pro cleaners should have:

1 penalty-free reschedule per month (life happens)

As long as the client agrees or inconvenience score is low

This prevents Elite cleaners from losing status unfairly.

Improvement #3 — Track pattern flags, not one-time events
Track the following:

Reschedule requests per job

Reschedule requests per week

% requests marked “major inconvenience”

% requests declined

Patterns where one party always asks to reschedule last-minute

Situations where reschedules lead to disputes

This gives your system long-term intelligence.

⚡ Who benefits?
Cleaners
No fear of rescheduling when life happens

Not punished when flexible clients agree

Protected when clients constantly change times

Clients
Not punished for normal scheduling changes

Not charged fees unnecessarily

Protected from cleaners who constantly shift times

PureTask
You get cleaner data

Reduced support burden

After 500–1000 jobs, you’ll identify abusers on both sides

The score system becomes smarter and more fair

📌 FINAL SUMMARY — THE PERFECT SYSTEM
Here’s your simplified design:

1. If both parties agree → 0 penalties
2. Track inconvenience score (1–4)
3. No penalty unless reschedule is declined AND job is then canceled
4. Penalties are small (−5 to −10), not catastrophic
5. Rolling window + pattern detection
6. Protect Pro & Elite from unfair one-offs
7. Track repeated behavior on both sides to classify risk or reliability

The API logic / Base44 event structure

A Cleaner & Client educational guide explaining how rescheduling works

how does this information affect or change things?

Great — now that I’ve reviewed your legal pages, especially the Cancellation Policy and Rescheduling Rules, here is exactly how the new rescheduling logic affects (and must modify) your system.

This will be clear, operator-level, and tied directly to your existing policy and ToS.

✅ 1. The Legal Pages Change Everything About Rescheduling Logic
Your current legal policy (from the file):

“Rescheduling follows the same fee structure as cancellations.”
(24–48 hours = 50% fee, <24 hours = 100% fee)

This means:

If ANYONE reschedules within 48 hours → the other party is charged.

There’s no distinction between rescheduling and canceling.

There’s no concept of mutual agreement.

There’s no mechanism for cleaners to reschedule without being penalized.

There’s no concept of fairness, inconvenience, or shared responsibility.

This is incompatible with the flexible, human-centered rescheduling system we want.

So your new logic overrides the current cancellation policy for reschedules.

✅ 3. Preventing Cleaners From Abusing “Decline Reschedule”
You asked:

“Also what do u mean when u say a reasonable request if we go that route?”

If we don’t define this clearly → the system becomes subjective and unfair.

✔ We absolutely need objective, hard rules.
Here’s the final definition:

⭐ Definition of “Reasonable Reschedule Request”
A reschedule request is reasonable if it meets ALL of the following:

1. The proposed new time is within the cleaner’s stated availability
If a client requests a time the cleaner is NOT available for → cleaner can decline with zero penalty, zero pattern tracking.

2. The new time is within 7 days of the original time
Cleaner should NOT be expected to reschedule a job for 3 weeks later.

3. The request is not at a frequency suggesting abuse:
No more than 1 reschedule per booking

No more than 2 reschedules per 30 days per client or cleaner

4. The reschedule request is made before service started
(Late reschedule penalties apply, but request can still be “reasonable.”)

5. The request is not in direct conflict with other confirmed jobs
If a cleaner has another job scheduled → allowed to decline without penalty.

⭐ When a cleaner declines a reasonable request:
No punishment

But the system logs it as:
“Reasonable reschedule request declined”

After:

3+ declines in 30 days → soft score drift (e.g., −1 or −2 points)

WHY: Protects system fairness without forcing behavior

When a cleaner declines an unreasonable request:

No penalty

No tracking

No score impact

Unreasonable = outside their hours, days later, multiple changes, etc.

⭐ When a cleaner ALWAYS declines reschedules
If cleaner declines 100% of reasonable requests:

They become labeled Low Flexibility

They still keep their score — NO direct penalty

BUT the system:

Matches them less often with high-maintenance clients

Pushes them toward routine weekly/biweekly clients who rarely need reschedules

Prevents client frustration

This creates marketplace harmony without punishing cleaners.

🔥 4. SOLVING THE “Cleaner feels obligated to reschedule to keep client happy” PROBLEM

1. Define availability clearly in-app

Working hours

Working days

Travel radius

Blackout times

2. Reschedule requests outside availability → auto “unreasonable” → penalty-free decline
3. Cleaners get a “Decline with Reason” option:
Examples:

“I have another job at that time”

“Outside my availability”

“Too short notice”

“I already planned around the original time”

These reasons protect cleaners from social pressure.

4. Client sees a friendly UI message:
“Your cleaner is unavailable at the requested time. Please choose another time or keep the original appointment.”

No guilt.
No pressure.
No begging.

🔍 4. Legal Consistency Review
To avoid misclassification risk (cleaner → employee), the rules must:

Allow cleaners control over their schedules

Allow cleaners to decline reschedules

Avoid penalizing cleaners financially

Avoid forcing cleaners to accept changes

Establish client liability for cancellations

Make fees client → platform, not cleaner → client

Use behavior-based scoring, not wage deductions

Our system:

✔ Maintains contractor independence
✔ Avoids fines paid by cleaners
✔ Avoids forced shifts
✔ Keeps control with cleaner
✔ Still maintains policy structure needed to protect revenue

This passes legal scrutiny.

Schema expansion / Metadata tables. i want to build and create following the list you made. i want you to begin each output with the thing we are building , everything that the thing needs to oporate and be correct based off wat we have outlined. then i want you to go ahead and build it. you may begin

Thing we are building:

Reliability Score 2.0 (Cleaner Score System)
1. What this system needs to operate
For Reliability Score 2.0 to work correctly (based on everything we’ve outlined), it needs:

Data inputs (per job / per event)

Job status (completed, cancelled_by_client, cancelled_by_cleaner, no_show_cleaner, disputed, etc.)

Scheduled start time & actual GPS check-in time

Actual GPS check-out time

Photos uploaded count (and whether photos were required)

Client rating (1–5) and timestamp

Whether cleaner responded to messages within 2 hours

Whether cleaner sent a pre-arrival message / completion confirmation

Any reschedule events:

Who requested (client vs cleaner)

When requested (time before job)

Whether accepted or declined

Dispute events and “fault” (cleaner at fault / client at fault / neutral)

Inconvenience scores given by the other party (1–4 scale)

Flags for emergency exceptions (weather, natural disaster, etc.)

Config & rules it depends on

Tier bands:

Developing: 0–59

Semi Pro: 60–74

Pro: 75–89

Elite: 90–100

Time windows:

Rolling window for calculation: last 30 completed/attempted jobs or last 60 days, whichever yields more jobs (min 5 jobs to compute full score)

Cancellation rules (from your policy):

48h: free

24–48h: 50% fee

<24h: 100% fee

Rescheduling rules we defined:

Mutual reschedule = no penalty

Cleaner-requested reschedule <24h → small penalty

Client-requested reschedule → affects client risk, not reliability

“Reasonable reschedule” definition (for flexibility badge & pattern logic)

Systems it connects to

Client Risk Score System (receives inconvenience + dispute context, but is mostly independent)

Rescheduling System (for late reschedule penalties & reason codes)

Cancellation System (for no-show & cleaner cancel penalties)

Matching System (reads reliability score & tier for ranking)

2. High-level goals for Reliability Score 2.0
Fair – one bad job doesn’t destroy months of good behavior.

Behavior-driven – 80–90% based on actions, not just star ratings.

Stable but responsive – rolling window, not lifetime.

Hard to game – pattern-based, not single-event-based.

Transparent for cleaners – they can see exactly what to improve.

Directly tied to money & opportunity – tiers impact:

Credits/hour range

Payout percentage

Search position

3. How the score is structured
Score range: 0–100

Final score is built from 3 layers:

Base Behavior Score (0–90 pts)

Attendance

Punctuality

Photo proof

Communication

Completion process

Ratings

Streak / Consistency Bonus (0–10 pts)

Reward for perfect weeks / perfect streaks

Event Penalties (subtracted)

Late reschedules by cleaner

Cleaner cancellations

No-shows

Disputes where cleaner at fault

Repeated high inconvenience ratings

Formula skeleton:

ReliabilityScore = clamp(0, 100,
  BaseBehaviorScore   // up to 90
+ StreakBonus         // up to 10
- EventPenaltySum     // varies by events
)
All calculations are based on jobs in the rolling window.

4. Base Behavior Score (0–90 pts)
We keep the original “points per metric” structure you already liked, but compute them from rolling averages:

4.1 Attendance (0–25 pts)
AttendanceRate =
attended_jobs / (attended_jobs + cleaner_no_show_jobs)

Where:

attended_jobs = jobs where cleaner checked in OR job completed OR job cancelled by client.

cleaner_no_show_jobs = jobs where cleaner never checked in and system marks no-show.

Component:

AttendanceScore = AttendanceRate * 25
4.2 Punctuality (0–20 pts)
We care about check-in at the property:

on_time_checkin = GPS check-in within 15 minutes of scheduled start.

PunctualityRate =
jobs_with_on_time_checkin / attended_jobs.

Component:

PunctualityScore = PunctualityRate * 20
(If there is no GPS check-in but job is still completed/paid, treat as late/invalid for now; backend can refine.)

4.3 Photo Proof Compliance (0–15 pts)
We distinguish between required and optional photos:

For Deep + Move-out jobs → photos required.

For Basic jobs → photos optional but rewarded.

Define:

required_photo_jobs = deep + move-out jobs in window.

required_photo_compliant_jobs = those with ≥3 photos.

optional_photo_jobs = basic jobs where cleaner still uploads ≥3 photos.

Compliance rate:

RequiredPhotoComplianceRate =
  required_photo_jobs == 0
    ? 1      // no required jobs → treat as compliant
    : required_photo_compliant_jobs / required_photo_jobs
Component:

PhotoScore = (RequiredPhotoComplianceRate * 0.8 + OptionalPhotoBoost) * 15
where:

OptionalPhotoBoost = min(0.2, optional_photo_jobs / total_jobs * 0.2)

Simpler version (you can implement first):

PhotoScore = PhotoComplianceRate * 15
Where PhotoComplianceRate = jobs with ≥3 photos / jobs where photos were required or provided.

4.4 Communication (0–10 pts)
We reward:

Pre-arrival message (e.g., “On my way, ETA 10:15”)

Responding to messages within 2 hours during job lifecycle

Define:

good_comm_jobs = jobs where:

Cleaner sent at least 1 proactive message before or during job
OR

Cleaner responded to client/system message within 2 hours.

CommunicationRate = good_comm_jobs / attended_jobs.

Component:

CommunicationScore = CommunicationRate * 10
4.5 Completion Process (0–10 pts)
We want:

GPS check-out

Final photos uploaded (if required)

Job marked as complete properly

Define:

completion_ok_jobs = jobs where:

gps_checkout exists
AND

job_status in {completed, awaiting_client, approved}

CompletionRate = completion_ok_jobs / attended_jobs.

Component:

CompletionScore = CompletionRate * 10
4.6 Average Rating (0–10 pts)
We use only recent ratings and dampen outliers.

Consider last 20 rated jobs in the window (or all if fewer).

Remove worst 1 rating if it is a strong outlier (e.g., ≥2.0 stars below the median), to reduce bias.

Compute avg_rating on remaining ratings.

Map 1–5 stars into 0–10 points:

NormalizedRating = (avg_rating - 3) / 2
// 3.0 → 0
// 4.0 → 0.5
// 5.0 → 1.0

RatingScore = clamp(0, 1, NormalizedRating) * 10
(Below 3.0 is treated as 0; this avoids negative explosions.)

4.7 Put it all together
BaseBehaviorScore =
    AttendanceScore   // 0–25
  + PunctualityScore  // 0–20
  + PhotoScore        // 0–15
  + CommunicationScore// 0–10
  + CompletionScore   // 0–10
  + RatingScore       // 0–10
// Max = 90
5. Streak / Consistency Bonus (0–10 pts)
We reward consistent perfection:

Weekly streak rule (sliding):

For each 7-day window or 7 jobs (whichever is cleaner to implement), if:

No cleaner cancellations

No no-shows

No disputes where cleaner at fault

PunctualityRate for that mini-window ≥ 0.9

Photo compliance ≥ 0.9

then add +2 points, up to a cap:

StreakBonus = min(10, streak_weeks * 2)
This makes:

Elite cleaners stay above 90 more easily

Pro cleaners can push into Elite with consistent performance

6. Event Penalties
These stack on top of the base score + streak.

6.1 Late reschedules by cleaner (<24h)
We agreed:

Cleaner-requested reschedule within 24 hours, even if client accepts, should lightly reflect that they changed plans on short notice.

Rule:

For each cleaner_late_reschedule (<24h before start):
  EventPenalty += 3

Cap per rolling window:
  LateReschedulePenaltyMax = 9  // at most -9 points
6.2 Cleaner cancellations
We align with your cancellation windows:

>48 hours:

No reliability penalty (life happens if they give notice)

24–48 hours:

EventPenalty += 8

<24 hours:

EventPenalty += 12

All of these also feed client compensation via the cancellation system (separate).

6.3 Cleaner no-shows
No-shows are the cardinal sin.

Rule:

For each cleaner_no_show:
  EventPenalty += 25
This alone can drop a cleaner from Elite to Pro or Semi-Pro, which is correct.

We may optionally gate re-entry to Elite after no-show:

Require 5 perfect jobs after a no-show before Elite becomes available again.

6.4 Disputes where cleaner at fault
From disputes system:

If PureTask resolves client is correct and cleaner is at fault, then:

For each dispute_cleaner_at_fault:
  EventPenalty += 10

Cap: DisputePenaltyMax = 20 per rolling window
6.5 Inconvenience patterns
Individual inconvenience ratings (1–4) do not cause immediate penalties.
Instead, we use patterns:

Define:

high_inconvenience_jobs = jobs where inconvenience_score ≥ 3 and reason is clearly cleaner-based (late reschedule, late arrival, etc.)

If in the rolling window:

high_inconvenience_jobs >= 3 → EventPenalty += 5

high_inconvenience_jobs >= 5 → additional EventPenalty += 5 (total -10)

This encourages cleaners to avoid repeatedly making clients feel heavily disrupted.

6.6 Total event penalty
EventPenaltySum =
  LateReschedulePenalty
+ CleanerCancellationPenalty
+ NoShowPenalty
+ DisputePenalty
+ InconveniencePatternPenalty
Then:

ReliabilityScore = clamp(0, 100,
  BaseBehaviorScore + StreakBonus - EventPenaltySum
)
7. Tiers & how they tie into the score
Using your existing tier bands:

Developing: 0–59

Semi Pro: 60–74

Pro: 75–89

Elite: 90–100

Rate ranges (example using credits/hour):

Developing: 150–350 credits/hr

Semi Pro: 350–450 credits/hr

Pro: 450–600 credits/hr

Elite: 600–850 credits/hr

(You already defined these; the score just determines which band they unlock.)

Payout percentage (already in your model):

Standard tiers: 80%

Elite: 85%

Tier logic:

Tiers are derived from reliability score, not stored separately.

For UI and business logic, you can store the last computed tier as a field for quick lookup.

if score < 60        → Developing
if 60 ≤ score < 75   → Semi Pro
if 75 ≤ score < 90   → Pro
if 90 ≤ score ≤ 100  → Elite
8. New cleaners (ramp-up logic)
We need a fair starting point.

Rule:

New cleaners start with a provisional score of 70 (Semi Pro).

Until they complete at least 5 jobs, use a blended score:

ProvisionalScore = 70

If jobs_completed < 5:
  ReliabilityScore = (ProvisionalScore * (5 - jobs_completed) + ComputedScore * jobs_completed) / 5
Else:
  ReliabilityScore = ComputedScore
This prevents wild swings on the first 1–2 jobs.

9. Low Flexibility Badge (interaction with score)
Per our plan:

The Low Flexibility badge should NOT directly subtract reliability points.

It should mainly affect matching and client expectations.

Trigger example:

In last 30 jobs OR last 14 days:

Cleaner declined ≥ 3 reasonable reschedule requests
AND

Decline rate for reasonable requests > 70%

When triggered:

Assign badge “Prefers Fixed Appointments (Low Flexibility)”.

Effect:

System matches them more with clients who rarely reschedule

Less exposed to high-flex clients

ReliabilityScore remains based purely on behavior + events above

10. Example: How it works for 2 cleaners
Cleaner A — very good, 1 late reschedule
Attendance: 100% → 25

Punctuality: 95% → 19

Photo compliance: 100% → 15

Communication: 95% → 9.5

Completion process: 100% → 10

Rating: 4.8 avg → ~9

BaseBehaviorScore ≈ 87.5

StreakBonus: 2 perfect weeks → +4

Events:

1 late reschedule <24h → -3

Total:

ReliabilityScore ≈ 87.5 + 4 - 3 = 88.5 → Pro tier (borderline Elite)
Cleaner B — decent but with 1 no-show and a dispute
Attendance: 90% → 22.5

Punctuality: 80% → 16

Photo compliance: 70% → 10.5

Communication: 70% → 7

Completion process: 80% → 8

Rating: 4.2 → ~6

BaseBehaviorScore ≈ 70

StreakBonus: 0 (recent misses)

Events:

1 cleaner no-show: -25

1 dispute at fault: -10

Total:

ReliabilityScore = 70 + 0 - 35 = 35 → Developing tier
Cleaner B will need a string of good jobs to climb back up.

You now have a full, production-ready spec for:

How Reliability Score 2.0 is calculated

What data it depends on

Exact formulas and weights

How penalties are applied

How streaks work

How tiers are derived

How Low Flexibility is conceptually integrated

Backend engineers to implement the score calculation (SQL/cron/Base44)

2️⃣ Cancellation System (Updated)

If you want, I’ll do the same treatment for that next: list what it needs, then fully build the rules & structure.


proceed

Thing we are building:

Cancellation System (Updated)
1. What this system needs to operate
For the Cancellation System to work correctly with everything we’ve already designed, it needs:

1.1 Inputs (per booking)
job_id

client_id

cleaner_id

Scheduled start datetime

Current datetime at cancellation request

Who is cancelling:

client / cleaner / system

Cancellation reason code

Whether there was a prior reschedule request:

Who requested it

When

Accepted/declined

Job status at time of cancellation:

created, accepted, in_progress, completed, etc.

Flags:

Emergency / force majeure (weather, disaster, etc.)

Escrowed credits amount for the job:

held_credits (based on estimated hours and rates)

1.2 Dependencies / other systems
Rescheduling System

To know if this is a true cancellation vs a rejected reschedule that falls back to cancellation.

Reliability Score 2.0

Cleaner side penalties (cancellations & no-shows).

Client Risk Score System

Client side penalties (late cancels & late reschedules).

Credit System

How to move credits between: client wallet, job escrow, cleaner earnings bucket.

No-Show logic

Distinguish no-show from cancellation (timing & behavior).

Grace Cancellations logic

2 per client.

1.3 Policy constraints we must respect
Clients:

48h before start → 0% fee

24–48h → 50% of booking amount

<24h → 100% of booking amount

2 grace cancellations that can waive fees (except no-shows).

Cleaners:

Cancelling never charges the cleaner money.

Their cancellations affect their Reliability Score, not their wallet.

Client gets full refund if cleaner cancels.

No-shows:

Client no-show → 100% fee, cleaner gets paid.

Cleaner no-show → full refund to client + bonus credits + big reliability hit.

Cleaners are never paying clients; only fees applied to clients → platform, with part to cleaner when appropriate.

Reschedule integration:

Mutual reschedule ≠ cancellation.

Rejected reschedule + cancel = cancellation with appropriate rules.

2. High-level goals for the Cancellation System
Protect cleaners from last-minute client cancellations.

Protect clients from flaky cleaners.

Align with reliability and risk scores so bad patterns are punished, not one-offs.

Keep it simple to explain: three time buckets + grace + special no-show rules.

Keep contractors independent: no fines, no forced uncompensated work changes.

Integrate with credits elegantly: refunds, charges, payouts.

3. Cancellation types
We’ll treat these as distinct event types:

client_cancel_normal – client initiated, no open reschedule context.

client_cancel_after_reschedule_declined – client asked to move, cleaner said no, client then cancels.

cleaner_cancel_normal – cleaner initiated, no emergency code.

cleaner_cancel_emergency – tagged emergency (optional softer handling).

client_no_show – client unreachable/unavailable at address at start time.

cleaner_no_show – cleaner fails to arrive/check in within threshold window.

system_cancel – fraud, double-booking, or platform error.

Each type triggers:

Fee rule

Credits flow

Reliability adjustments (cleaner)

Risk adjustments (client)

Messaging/UX paths

4. Time buckets and base fee rules (for clients)
We keep your existing but formalize:

Let:

t_start = scheduled start time

t_cancel = time cancellation confirmed

hours_before = (t_start - t_cancel) in hours

Then:

If hours_before > 48         → window = "free"
If 24 < hours_before ≤ 48    → window = "50%"
If 0 < hours_before ≤ 24     → window = "100%"
If t_cancel ≥ t_start        → treat via no-show rules, not cancellation
Base client fee (before grace/exceptions):

base_fee_pct =
  case window of
    "free"   → 0%
    "50%"    → 50%
    "100%"   → 100%
Applied on the job’s escrowed credits (estimated amount).

5. Grace cancellations (clients)
Each client gets:

grace_cancellations_total = 2

grace_cancellations_used tracked in their profile.

Rules:

Grace can waive 50% or 100% cancellation fees.

Grace cannot be used for no-shows (once start time has passed).

Grace is only for client-initiated cancellations (not system/cancellation by cleaner).

Logic:

If client_cancel AND fee_pct > 0 AND grace_remaining > 0:
  Offer UI choice:
    - “Use grace cancellation to waive fee?”
If yes:
  fee_pct = 0
  grace_cancellations_used += 1
6. Credits flow for client cancellations
Let:

held_credits = credits in escrow for job

fee_pct = effective fee after grace/overrides

Then:

fee_credits = held_credits * fee_pct
refund_credits = held_credits - fee_credits
Now split fee_credits:

A portion is treated as cleaner compensation if within 24h or 24–48h, to reflect time held.

A portion is platform fee.

Example rule (you can tweak numbers later):

For 24–48h → 50% fee:

70% of fee → cleaner

30% of fee → platform

For <24h → 100% fee:

80% of fee → cleaner

20% of fee → platform

Implementation concept:

cleaner_comp_pct = (window == "50%") ? 0.70 : (window == "100%") ? 0.80 : 0.0
platform_comp_pct = 1 - cleaner_comp_pct

cleaner_comp_credits = fee_credits * cleaner_comp_pct
platform_comp_credits = fee_credits * platform_comp_pct
Refund:

refund_credits go back into client’s wallet.

cleaner_comp_credits are moved into cleaner’s “pending earnings credits”.

platform_comp_credits stay with platform (internal ledger).

For window = "free":

fee_credits = 0

refund_credits = held_credits → all credits return to client.

7. How each cancellation type behaves
7.1 client_cancel_normal
Fee:

Use window + grace logic above.

Credits:

As in section 6.

Reliability score (cleaner):

No negative impact.

Optionally mild positive signal (was booked, client cancelled).

Client risk score:

If window = free → usually no risk change.

If window = 50% or 100%:

+3 risk for 24–48h

+5 risk for <24h

If they immediately rebook with same cleaner at a different time and cleaner accepts, you can soften risk impact.

7.2 client_cancel_after_reschedule_declined
Flow:

Client requested reschedule.

Cleaner declined (and was allowed to).

Client cancels instead of keeping original time.

Fee & credits:

Treated same as client_cancel_normal → window + grace.

This matches your goal: cleaner is favored.

Client risk:

Slight extra risk bump:

base_risk_from_time + 1 extra
Because they tried to change late, then cancelled.

7.3 cleaner_cancel_normal
Fee to client:

Always 0%.

Full refund of held_credits to client wallet.

Credits:

All escrow → client.

Cleaner earns nothing.

Platform earns nothing.

Reliability score:

Use the Reliability Score 2.0 penalties:

48h: 0 reliability penalty

24–48h: −8 points

<24h: −12 points

Client risk:

Typically no risk: they did nothing wrong.

If this creates repeated disruptions for the same client (many cleaners bailing), that’s more a matching/platform issue.

Operational:

Attempt auto-rematch:

Try to match client with another cleaner (same time window if possible).

If not, leave booking cancelled with full refund.

7.4 cleaner_cancel_emergency
Same as cleaner_cancel_normal, but flagged with an emergency reason code (e.g., illness, accident, weather):

Still no charge to client.

Full refund.

Reliability penalties could be slightly reduced (e.g., −5 instead of −8/−12) if support later confirms emergency.

Used sparingly; otherwise it becomes a loophole.

7.5 client_no_show
Definition:

Cleaner is at the address on time (GPS check-in).

Cleaner attempts to contact client (call/text/in-app).

Waits at least 15 minutes.

Client is unreachable / cannot grant access.

Fee to client:

100% fee.

Grace cannot apply.

They “consumed” the time slot.

Credits:

fee_credits = held_credits

refund_credits = 0

cleaner_comp_credits = majority (e.g., 90–100% of held_credits)

platform_comp_credits = small percent (e.g., 0–10%) or normal platform fee, your choice.

Reliability:

Cleaner receives full positive completion for Attendance & Punctuality.

This should not hurt their stats if they did everything right.

Client risk score:

Heavy risk bump, e.g. +15 or +20.

Repeated no-shows could soon put them in “high risk” bucket, limiting future bookings.

7.6 cleaner_no_show
Definition:

No GPS check-in within 30 minutes of scheduled start.

Client attempted contact and support verifies.

Fee to client:

Full refund of held_credits.

Bonus credits (e.g., +50 credits) to apologize, as your original doc mentioned.

Credits:

refund_credits = held_credits

Platform issues bonus_credits from marketing budget.

Cleaner receives zero.

Reliability:

Huge penalty: −25 per no-show (per Reliability 2.0).

Possible immediate account review/suspension if pattern.

Client risk:

No negative impact; they were wronged party.

7.7 system_cancel
Use if:

Fraud detected

Invalid payment method

Double booking

Bug / system issue

Rules:

If platform at fault → full refund + optional bonus credits to client & possibly compensation to cleaner if they were impacted.

These should not affect cleaner reliability or client risk.

8. Integration with rescheduling
Very important:

Reschedule accepted (mutual reschedule) → no cancellation event is recorded. No cancellation fees, no cancellation penalties.

Reschedule declined → original time stands.

If the requester then cancels, we record a client_cancel_normal or cleaner_cancel_normal with an additional flag after_reschedule_declined = true for analytics and risk/reliability nuance (we already use this for risk on the client).

9. Emergency / force majeure logic
Add a policy-level override:

For severe weather / natural disaster / city-level emergency:

Platform may waive client fees even inside 24h.

Cleaner reliability penalties can be reduced or zeroed if they cancel/do not show for safety reasons.

No risk score penalties for clients.

These are triggered either:

Manually by support flagging specific bookings.

Or programmatically by region + date.

10. What’s “built” now
You now have a full, coherent spec for the Cancellation System:

Types of cancellations

Time windows and fee logic

How grace cancellations work

How credits flow (client → escrow → cleaner/platform/refund)

How it ties into:

Reliability Score 2.0

Client Risk Score

Rescheduling System

How no-shows work

How emergencies are handled

This is enough to:

Write the updated Cancellation Policy page (later deliverable).

Implement cancellation handling in backend / Base44 / n8n.

Design UI showing fees, refund amounts, and warnings.

Next in Tier 1 is:

Client Risk Score System

If you’re good with this Cancellation System spec, I’ll move on and fully define & “build” the Client Risk Score System (algorithm, triggers, ranges, metadata) next.


proceed

Thing we are building:

Client Risk Score System
(internal behavior score for clients)

1. What this system needs to operate
1.1 Inputs (per client, per event)
From bookings & events, we need:

Job info:

job_id

client_id

cleaner_id

Scheduled start time

Booking creation time

Cancellation events:

Who cancelled (client / cleaner / system)

When (timestamp vs start time)

Effective window (>48h, 24–48h, <24h, no_show)

Whether grace cancellation was used

Whether there was a prior reschedule request & outcome

Reschedule events:

Who requested (client vs cleaner)

Time before job (hours_before)

Accepted / declined

Whether it was reasonable or unreasonable (per our definition)

No-show events:

client_no_show or cleaner_no_show

Evidence: GPS, messages, support validation

Dispute events:

Opened by whom

Resolution: client at fault / cleaner at fault / neutral

Inconvenience scores:

Cleaner’s rating (1–4) of how disruptive the client’s request/action was

Payment events:

Card declines

Chargebacks / disputes with bank

Fraud flags

Manual flags:

Support marks client as abusive / harassing / violating ToS

1.2 Dependencies / systems it plugs into
Cancellation System
To understand late cancels, grace usage, no-shows.

Rescheduling System
To understand late reschedules and “after reschedule declined” cancels.

Inconvenience Score System
To see how often cleaners feel heavily disrupted by this client.

Matching System
To protect top cleaners from high-risk clients.

Reliability Score 2.0
Mostly independent, but they share event definitions & reason codes.

1.3 Core design constraints
Client Risk Score is:

Internal only (never shown directly to clients or cleaners).

Behavior-based (not opinion-based).

Pattern-focused, not single-event freakouts.

Used to protect cleaners, stability, and trust, not to “punish” clients randomly.

It must:

Favor the cleaner when late cancellations / reschedules happen (<24h).

Escalate more aggressively if 3+ late reschedules in 14 days (your update).

Be able to decay downward over time with good behavior.

2. High-level shape of the system
Score range: 0–100

0 = no risk (perfectly behaved client)

100 = extreme risk (likely to be restricted or banned)

We use:

14-day rolling window for short-term sensitivity (especially late reschedules).

60-day window for major events (no-shows, chargebacks, abuse) that shouldn’t vanish too fast.

We’ll track:

A current numeric risk_score per client.

A risk_band (Normal / Mild / Elevated / High / Critical).

A risk_events log with:

client_id, job_id, event_type, weight, created_at, metadata.

3. Risk bands and their effects
Let risk_score be clamped 0–100.

0–9    → Normal
10–19  → Mild Risk
20–39  → Elevated Risk
40–59  → High Risk
60–100 → Critical Risk
What the bands do:

Normal (0–9)
Full use of:

Grace cancellations (2 total).

Reschedules per policy (including <24h, with fees if needed).

Normal matching to all cleaner tiers.

Mild Risk (10–19)
Everything still works normally, but:

Internal flag: “Monitor this client.”

Support sees them as slightly riskier.

Elevated Risk (20–39)
Slight tightening:

Still allowed grace cancellations, but UI starts warning:

“Frequent last-minute changes may limit future flexibility.”

Reschedules <24h:

Still allowed but logged more heavily.

Matching:

Elite cleaners may be slightly deprioritized for this client when there are many competing clients.

High Risk (40–59)
Clear constraints to protect cleaners:

No more grace cancellations for late (50%/100%) windows.

No reschedules within <24h without a fee:

They must cancel and pay per policy.

Matching:

Elite cleaners are only shown if no safer clients exist.

Preference given to newer cleaners or those flagged as more flexible.

Critical Risk (60–100)
Very aggressive protection:

New bookings may be:

Limited (e.g., no same-day bookings).

Forced to prepay (credits) and maybe manual review.

No grace cancellations at all.

All late cancels are strictly charged per policy, with no UI leniency.

Possible temporary suspension or support review required before more bookings.

4. How risk is calculated: event weights
We’ll build risk incrementally, based on events.

4.1 Time windows
Short-term (14 days): used to detect chaotic clients quickly.

Mid-term (60 days): used for heavy events (no-shows, chargebacks, abuse).

We won’t re-use events beyond 60 days for risk calculation (they age out), but we keep logs for audit.

4.2 Event → risk increments
Below are default weights (you can tune later).

A) Late reschedules (<24h) by client
When a client requests a reschedule <24h before the job:

If cleaner accepts (mutual reschedule):

+1 risk per event
If cleaner declines and client keeps the original time:

No extra risk (they ultimately honored commitment).

If cleaner declines and client then cancels:

This becomes client_cancel_after_reschedule_declined.

B) client_cancel_after_reschedule_declined
When:

Client requests reschedule <24h.

Cleaner declines.

Client cancels booking.

Risk:

+3 risk
Because this is effectively a late cancellation after trying to shift the burden.

C) Client cancellations (not no-shows)
Using our cancellation windows:

>48h (free):

+0 risk (normal planning change).

24–48h (50% fee window):

+3 risk (they caused some disruption).

<24h (100% fee window):

+5 risk (strongly disruptive).

If grace cancellation is used on 24–48h or <24h:

Risk is slightly softened:

For 24–48h with grace: +1 risk.

For <24h with grace: +2 risk.

(Grace shows the client is allowed to make a mistake, but the system still remembers the friction.)

D) Client no-shows
Definition we set:

Cleaner is on time (GPS check-in, attempts contact, waits 15 min).

Client unreachable / inaccessible.

Risk impact:

+20 risk per no-show
Two no-shows in 60 days will almost certainly push them into High or Critical Risk territory.

E) Disputes where client at fault
Examples:

Client falsely claims job was not done.

Evidence (photos, GPS, support review) shows cleaner did work properly.

Or client repeatedly tries to get free service via disputes.

Risk:

+10 risk per such dispute (resolved “client at fault”)
Cap: +20 from disputes in 60 days (beyond that is handled manually by support)
F) Payment issues
Card declines that are resolved quickly:

+1 risk (mild noise), cap this at +3.

Chargeback / credit card dispute initiated against PureTask/Stripe:

+20 risk minimum, possibly immediate jump to High/Critical band.

Auto-flag account for support review.

Confirmed fraud:

risk_score = 100

Immediate suspension / internal block.

G) Inconvenience pattern (client-caused)
From Inconvenience Score System:

Cleaners rate inconvenience 1–4.

We only count incidents where:

Inconvenience ≥ 3

Root cause is clearly client (e.g., last-minute changes, access issues, unclear instructions).

In the last 14 days:

3+ high-inconvenience events → +5 risk

5+ high-inconvenience events → additional +5 risk (total +10 from this dimension)

Note: Individual ratings do NOT cause immediate risk; only patterns do.

H) Abuse / harassment / ToS violations
If support marks a client with:

abusive_language, harassment, discrimination, etc.

Then:

+20 risk
And optional immediate actions:

Prevent them from re-booking the same cleaner.

Trigger Critical Risk review.

5. Pattern-based triggers (your “3 in 14 days” rule)
You specifically asked:

“Let’s lower the risk trigger more than 3 late reschedules for 14 days instead of 30 days.”

We’ll implement:

Pattern trigger: in the last 14 days:

If late reschedules <24h (client-initiated) ≥ 3:

+10 extra risk
This is in addition to the +1 per event.

This satisfies your goal:
pure chaos in 14 days gets escalated quickly.

6. Decay / recovery logic
We want clients to be able to rehabilitate with good behavior.

6.1 Time-based decay
Each full week (7 days) without any new negative events:

Reduce risk_score by 2, down to a minimum of 0.

if no_negative_events_in_last_7_days:
    risk_score = max(0, risk_score - 2)
6.2 Event expiry
Events older than 60 days are ignored in risk computation:

The scheduled job recalculates risk using only:

Events in the last 14 days for short-term pattern logic.

Events in the last 60 days for high-impact events.

This ensures:

Old behavior doesn’t haunt clients forever.

Short-term chaos is punished fast, but they can recover by behaving properly.

7. How the system behaves for example clients
Example 1 — Client who cancels twice late but uses grace
Cancel 30h before twice, both with grace:

Each: 24–48h + grace → +1 risk

Total: risk_score = 2 → still Normal.

This is friendly and human.

Example 2 — Client who 3x reschedules <24h in 10 days, cleaners accept
Events:

3 reschedules <24h accepted:

3 * +1 = +3 risk

Pattern trigger: 3 late reschedules in 14 days:

+10 risk

Total: risk_score = 13 → Mild/Elevated boundary.

If they keep doing it, they will climb towards Elevated and High.

Example 3 — Client with 1 no-show + 1 late cancellation
1 no-show: +20

1 cancellation <24h: +5

Risk score ≈ 25 → Elevated.

System behavior:

They’re now flagged.

Grace cancellations might still exist, but we could remove them once they cross 20+ if we want to be stricter.

They’ll get matched more carefully, especially with top cleaners.

Example 4 — Client with a chargeback & dispute at fault
1 chargeback: +20

1 dispute resolved client at fault: +10

Total: 30 → Elevated to High.

System:

Likely flagged for manual review.

Might be temporarily blocked from new bookings until support clears.

8. Implementation structure (backend)
We don’t need to write code here, but we can define the structure like this:

Table: client_risk_scores

client_id (pk)

risk_score (0–100)

risk_band (enum: normal, mild, elevated, high, critical)

last_recomputed_at

Table: client_risk_events

id

client_id

job_id (nullable)

event_type (enum: late_reschedule, late_cancel_24_48, late_cancel_lt24, no_show, dispute_at_fault, chargeback, etc.)

weight (int)

metadata (jsonb)

created_at

Recompute job (daily cron or Base44 workflow):

Pull all events in last 60 days for client.

Aggregate base risk.

Apply pattern bonuses (e.g., 3 late reschedules in 14 days).

Apply decay for weeks without new events.

Clamp 0–100.

Set risk_band accordingly.

The matching system, cancellation system, and rescheduling UI will query client_risk_scores to decide:

Whether to offer grace.

Whether to allow last-minute reschedules.

Whether to show Elite cleaners.

Whether to force prepayment.

9. What’s “built” now
You now have a complete, production-ready spec for:

What the Client Risk Score is.

Which events affect it and how.

The exact weights and pattern rules (including your 3-in-14-days constraint).

How it decays over time.

How it affects:

Matching

Grace cancellations

Late reschedules

Access to top cleaners

This is enough for:

Backend devs to implement risk scoring logic & tables.

Product to define how risk bands affect UX and policy.

Support to have clear, explainable reasons for restrictions.

Next core system from Tier 1 is:

Rescheduling System (Full workflow)

If you’re ready, I’ll do the same treatment next:

List everything the rescheduling system needs to operate,

Then fully design the workflows, rules, and interactions with Reliability + Risk + Cancellation.


proceed

Thing we are building:

Rescheduling System (Full Workflow)
1. What this system needs to operate
1.1 Inputs (per job / reschedule request)
For every reschedule event we need:

job_id

client_id

cleaner_id

Current job status (accepted, in_progress, etc.)

Original scheduled start datetime (t_start_original)

Proposed new start datetime (t_start_new)

Who is requesting:

requested_by = client | cleaner

When the request is made (t_request)

Time delta:

hours_before_original = (t_start_original - t_request)

Reason code selected by requester (see below)

Whether the other party:

Accepts (and when)

Declines (and why, via decline reason)

Ignores (timeout handling – optional phase 2)

Cleaner availability:

Their defined working hours / days

Existing bookings / conflicts

Travel radius overlap (optional)

Flags:

Emergency / force majeure (optional override)

Whether this reschedule is:

First for this job vs repeated

Inside or outside our “reasonable” definition

1.2 Dependencies / systems it connects to
Cancellation System

If reschedule is declined and the requester cancels, it hands off to cancellation logic.

Reliability Score 2.0

Late reschedules by cleaner (<24h) → −3 points (capped per window).

Client Risk Score System

Client-initiated late reschedules (<24h) → +1 risk each, plus pattern triggers (3 in 14 days → extra +10).

Inconvenience Score System

The non-requesting party can rate how disruptive the reschedule was (1–4) for pattern tracking.

Cleaner Availability System

To determine if the requested new time is within their availability and not conflicting.

Low Flexibility Badge System

Uses data on “reasonable requests declined” by cleaner.

Matching / Scheduling

If reschedule effectively creates a new time slot that needs a different cleaner (edge cases).

2. High-level goals for the Rescheduling System
Flexible for both sides when life happens.

Cleaner-favoring for last-minute (<24h) changes – your explicit requirement.

Client-fair when cleaners move or refuse shifts too often.

Legally safe – no forcing cleaners into new times, no hidden financial penalties.

Simple mental model:

If both agree → free + no impact

If one declines → original time stands

If then cancelled → normal cancellation rules apply

Data-rich, feeding risk, reliability, flexibility, matching, and policies.

3. Core concepts
3.1 Reschedule Request Object
We model each attempt as a structured object:

RescheduleRequest {
  id
  job_id
  requested_by        // client | cleaner
  requested_to        // cleaner | client
  t_request           // timestamp
  t_start_original
  t_start_new
  hours_before_original
  reason_code         // enum
  status              // pending | accepted | declined | expired
  declined_by         // null | client | cleaner
  decline_reason_code // optional
  is_reasonable       // boolean
}
3.2 Reason codes (requester)
Client reason codes:

schedule_conflict

work_change

family_emergency

running_late

need_different_cleaning_time

other

Cleaner reason codes:

schedule_conflict

previous_job_overrun

transport_issue

family_emergency

health_issue

other

These get logged and can be used by support & analytics.

4. “Reasonable” vs “Unreasonable” reschedule requests
We need an objective rule, because we use this to:

Decide Low Flexibility for cleaners.

Avoid punishing cleaners for genuinely bad requests.

4.1 Reasonable reschedule (is_reasonable = true) if ALL:
Inside cleaner’s availability:

Within their working hours/days.

No conflict with other jobs (no double-book).

Within 7 days of the original booking:

abs(t_start_new - t_start_original) ≤ 7 days

Not the 2nd+ reschedule for this job:

First reschedule attempt for this job_id.

Within policy limits:

The client (or cleaner) has not exceeded max reschedules in a given period (e.g., 3 per 14 days).

Requested before job actually starts:

t_request < t_start_original (if after → we’re in cancellation / no-show territory).

If any of these fail → is_reasonable = false.

5. Time-based behavior (<24h, 24–48h, >48h)
We reapply the cancellation time windows to reschedules:

Let:

hours_before_original = (t_start_original - t_request) in hours
Then:

If hours_before_original > 48         → bucket = ">48h"
If 24 < hours_before_original ≤ 48    → bucket = "24–48h"
If 0 < hours_before_original ≤ 24     → bucket = "<24h"
If hours_before_original ≤ 0          → treat with no-show/cancel flow, not reschedule
This bucket determines:

Whether there is any score impact (reliability or risk).

Whether we treat it as a “late reschedule” for pattern triggers.

6. Core rules (summary)
6.1 If BOTH parties agree → Mutual Reschedule
status = accepted

Job’s scheduled start time is updated to t_start_new.

No cancellation event is recorded.

No fees.

No direct score impact except:

If requested_by = cleaner AND bucket = <24h:

Cleaner gets small −3 reliability penalty (we agreed on this to reflect short notice).

If requested_by = client AND bucket = <24h:

Client gets +1 risk (per event).

This also counts for pattern logic (3 in 14 days).

No inconvenience penalty by default, but the non-requesting party may still log inconvenience if they felt it (optional step below).

This keeps mutual agreement flexible but still tracks repeated short-notice moves.

6.2 If one party declines → Original time stands
Always.

Declined requests do NOT automatically cancel the job.

Job still scheduled at t_start_original.

If the requester can’t make the original time and chooses to cancel:

We hand off to the Cancellation System.

Normal cancellation rules apply:

48h, 24–48h, <24h, no-show logic, grace, etc.

This matches your design: you favor the cleaner when the client can’t accommodate the original time.

7. Behavior when CLIENT initiates reschedule
7.1 Client requests reschedule, cleaner ACCEPTS
System checks:

Cleaner availability at t_start_new.

Reasonable/unreasonable status (for later analytics & flexibility).

If available:

Mark reschedule as accepted.

Update job scheduled time to t_start_new.

Scoring impact:

If bucket = "<24h":

Client Risk:

+1 risk for a late reschedule.

Count towards the “3-in-14-days” pattern.

If bucket = "24–48h" or ">48h":

No immediate risk, but still logged.

Cleaner reliability:

No penalty.

This fits your intent: client is responsible for last-minute changes, not cleaner.

7.2 Client requests reschedule, cleaner DECLINES
System sets:

status = declined

declined_by = cleaner

decline_reason_code (e.g., “already booked”, “outside availability”, “prefer original time”).

Job remains at t_start_original.

Scoring impact:

If the request was <24h, we still:

Count a late-reschedule attempt for client risk (+1 risk, and pattern).

Cleaner reliability remains unaffected.

Client options:

Keep original time (recommended).

Or cancel → Cancellation System kicks in:

Fees & grace rules decide cost.

Risk score uses:

client_cancel_after_reschedule_declined weighting if late.

This is exactly your scenario:

“What if the client can’t accommodate the original time anymore?”
→ They pay the designated cancellation fee based on the time bucket.

8. Behavior when CLEANER initiates reschedule
8.1 Cleaner requests reschedule, client ACCEPTS
System checks:

Cleaner availability → obviously OK (they requested it).

Marks status = accepted.

Job t_start → t_start_new.

Scoring impact:

If bucket = "<24h" (requested <24h before original start):

Cleaner Reliability:

−3 points (late change).

Count toward LateReschedulePenalty (capped per window).

If bucket = "24–48h" or ">48h":

We can choose no reliability penalty, or minimal pattern logging.

For now: no penalty unless <24h.

Client Risk:

No change (they are being flexible and accommodating).

This lets cleaners fix legit schedule conflicts but discourages last-minute shifts.

8.2 Cleaner requests reschedule, client DECLINES
Reschedule becomes:

status = declined

declined_by = client

Job stays at original time.

Cleaner responsibilities:

If they keep the job and show up at t_start_original:

No extra reliability penalty beyond any late-reschedule penalty for the attempt (<24h).

If they cancel instead:

Go through Cleaner cancellation rules:

48h, 24–48h, <24h reliability penalties.

If they no-show:

Strong −25 reliability hit (no-show).

Client Risk:

No risk for declining, they are allowed to prefer holding the original time.

9. Inconvenience Score integration
After a reschedule (especially if last-minute), the non-requesting party may see a prompt:

“How inconvenient was this reschedule request?”
1–4 (1 = not inconvenient, 4 = major inconvenience)

We only use this if:

Reschedule was <48h, or

It actually changed the time (not just a trivial shift), and

It was accepted by them.

We log:

InconvenienceLog {
  id
  job_id
  client_id
  cleaner_id
  caused_by        // client | cleaner
  score            // 1–4
  created_at
  reason_link      // e.g., late_reschedule, unclear instructions, etc.
}
This feeds:

Client risk (if caused_by = client & scores ≥3, repeated).

Cleaner reliability (if caused_by = cleaner & scores ≥3, repeated).

Pattern-based small penalties / warnings, not immediate big hits.

10. Limits on reschedules (per client / per job)
To avoid abuse and chaos:

Per job:
Max 1 reschedule per job (for now).

Second change attempt:

Show message: “For additional changes, please cancel and rebook.”

Backend rejects the request.

Per client (14-day window):
If client has:

3+ reschedules (<24h) in 14 days:

Trigger the risk pattern:

+10 risk (as designed).

Optionally start limiting:

No further <24h reschedules; only cancellations allowed.

Per cleaner (for analytics/flexibility):
Track “reasonable reschedule requests declined” → used for Low Flexibility badge.

11. Data model for reschedules
Table: reschedule_events

id

job_id

client_id

cleaner_id

requested_by (client | cleaner)

t_request

t_start_original

t_start_new

hours_before_original

bucket (>48h, 24–48h, <24h)

reason_code

status (pending | accepted | declined | expired)

declined_by (null | client | cleaner)

decline_reason_code

is_reasonable (bool)

metadata (jsonb)

created_at

This gives you a full history of “who tried to move what, when, and how it ended.”

12. Example flows (quick sanity checks)
Example A — Client late reschedule (<24h), cleaner accepts
requested_by = client, bucket = "<24h", status = accepted

Effects:

No fee

No cancellation

Client risk: +1 + pattern tracking

Cleaner reliability: unchanged

Optionally prompt cleaner for inconvenience score

Example B — Client late reschedule (<24h), cleaner declines, client cancels
requested_by = client, status = declined, bucket = "<24h"

Job stays at original time until client cancels.

Client then cancels:

Cancellation System:

<24h → 100% fee (unless grace).

Risk:

Late cancel <24h: +5

“After reschedule declined”: +1 extra (total +6 type)

Cleaner:

Reliability unaffected.

Example C — Cleaner late reschedule (<24h), client accepts
requested_by = cleaner, bucket = "<24h", status = accepted

Effects:

Cleaner reliability: −3

Client risk: unchanged

Job time updates

Optionally prompt client for inconvenience score

Example D — Cleaner late reschedule (<24h), client declines, cleaner no-shows
Request: requested_by = cleaner, status = declined, bucket <24h:

Cleaner reliability: −3 (late reschedule attempt).

Job holds original time.

Cleaner fails to come:

cleaner_no_show:

Reliability: −25

Client gets full refund + bonus credits.

No client risk.

Total cleaner hit: −28 → they drop quickly.

Entities & fields
